# Create the Mene Long-Term Memory (M-LTM) project with runnable code and Docker setup
import os, textwrap, json, zipfile, pathlib, datetime, shutil, sys, io, random

base = "/mnt/data/mene-ltm"
if os.path.exists(base):
    shutil.rmtree(base)
os.makedirs(base, exist_ok=True)

# Directory structure
dirs = [
    "api/app",
    "cli",
]
for d in dirs:
    os.makedirs(os.path.join(base, d), exist_ok=True)

# .env.example
env_example = """\
# ==== Database ====
POSTGRES_HOST=db
POSTGRES_PORT=5432
POSTGRES_DB=mene_mem
POSTGRES_USER=mene
POSTGRES_PASSWORD=change_me

# ==== Embeddings ====
# Options: fastembed, openai
EMBED_PROVIDER=fastembed
# For fastembed: BAAI/bge-small-en-v1.5 (384-dim) is the default & CPU-friendly
EMBED_MODEL=BAAI/bge-small-en-v1.5
# For openai: set OPENAI_API_KEY and EMBED_MODEL=text-embedding-3-small (or similar)
OPENAI_API_KEY=
OPENAI_BASE_URL=https://api.openai.com/v1

# ==== Consolidation / Summarization (optional) ====
# If you want server-side summarization/consolidation via an LLM HTTP endpoint,
# set LLM_URL to a compatible /v1/chat/completions or OpenAI-compatible endpoint.
LLM_URL=
LLM_API_KEY=
LLM_MODEL=gpt-4o-mini

# ==== API ====
API_PORT=8000
API_HOST=0.0.0.0
API_AUTH_TOKEN=dev_token_change_me

# ==== Recall ====
# Max memories to return per recall
RECALL_TOP_K=15
# Boost for importance (0-3) in scoring
IMPORTANCE_BOOST=0.2
# Recency half-life in days (higher = slower decay)
RECENCY_HALF_LIFE_DAYS=14

# ==== Storage ====
# Where to store model cache in the container
MODEL_CACHE=/root/.cache/mene_ltm
"""
open(os.path.join(base, ".env.example"), "w").write(env_example)

# README.md
readme = """\
# Mene Long-Term Memory (M-LTM) â€” v0.1

Local, sovereign, long-term memory service for AI agents. FastAPI + Postgres/pgvector with CPU-friendly embeddings.

## What it gives you

- **Episodic + Semantic memory** stored locally.
- **Salience scoring** (importance, recency, usage).
- **Vector recall** via pgvector + hybrid rerank.
- **REST API**: `POST /event`, `GET /recall`, `POST /reinforce/{id}`, `POST /forget/{id}`, `GET /dump`.
- **Pluggable embeddings**: local `fastembed` (BAAI/bge-small-en-v1.5) by default or OpenAI-compatible.
- **Optional consolidation** (summarize, merge, compress) via your LLM endpoint.

## Quick start

```bash
# 1) Copy env and edit
cp .env.example .env
# Change POSTGRES_PASSWORD and API_AUTH_TOKEN at minimum

# 2) Up it
docker compose up -d --build

# 3) Health check
curl -H "Authorization: Bearer DEV_TOKEN_HERE" http://localhost:8000/health
```

## Write an event

```bash
curl -X POST http://localhost:8000/event \\
  -H "Authorization: Bearer DEV_TOKEN_HERE" \\
  -H "Content-Type: application/json" \\
  -d '{
    "content": "Booked Kitesurf repair at Durban North for Friday 10am",
    "type": "episodic",
    "tags": ["kitesurf","repair","schedule"],
    "source": "mene_portal",
    "importance": 2
  }'
```

## Recall

```bash
curl "http://localhost:8000/recall?q=kitesurf repair" \\
  -H "Authorization: Bearer DEV_TOKEN_HERE"
```

## Integrate with Meneâ€™ Portal (pseudocode)

Call `/event` for salient turns (tasks, decisions, facts), and call `/recall?q=<goal or user>` before planning:

```python
# before planning a response
mems = GET /recall?q="{user_goal or current_topic}"
# construct system/context with mem summaries
# after response, if new facts/commitments => POST /event
```

## Data model (simplified)

- `memories`: id, type, content, summary, importance, tags[], source, created_at, last_accessed, recall_count, embedding, decay_weight.
- **Types**: `episodic` (events), `semantic` (facts/skills), `procedural` (how-tos), `identity` (self/user profiles), `social` (people, prefs).
- **Scoring**: similarity Ã— Î± + importance Ã— Î² + recencyWeight Ã— Î³ + tagMatch bonus.

## Security

- Local only by default (binds to `0.0.0.0:8000`); use firewall or reverse proxy if exposing.
- Bearer token required (set `API_AUTH_TOKEN`).
- DB runs in your Docker; you own the data.

## Compose services

- `db`: Postgres with pgvector.
- `api`: FastAPI service.

## License

MIT. Use it, fork it, ship it.
"""
open(os.path.join(base, "README.md"), "w").write(readme)

# docker-compose.yml

services:
  db:
    image: ankane/pgvector:latest
    environment:
      POSTGRES_DB: ${POSTGRES_DB}
      POSTGRES_USER: ${POSTGRES_USER}
      POSTGRES_PASSWORD: ${POSTGRES_PASSWORD}
    ports:
      - "5432:5432"
    volumes:
      - pgdata:/var/lib/postgresql/data
    healthcheck:
      test: ["CMD-SHELL", "pg_isready -U $$POSTGRES_USER -d $$POSTGRES_DB"]
      interval: 5s
      timeout: 5s
      retries: 20

  api:
    build:
      context: ./api
    environment:
      - POSTGRES_HOST=${POSTGRES_HOST}
      - POSTGRES_PORT=${POSTGRES_PORT}
      - POSTGRES_DB=${POSTGRES_DB}
      - POSTGRES_USER=${POSTGRES_USER}
      - POSTGRES_PASSWORD=${POSTGRES_PASSWORD}
      - EMBED_PROVIDER=${EMBED_PROVIDER}
      - EMBED_MODEL=${EMBED_MODEL}
      - OPENAI_API_KEY=${OPENAI_API_KEY}
      - OPENAI_BASE_URL=${OPENAI_BASE_URL}
      - API_PORT=${API_PORT}
      - API_HOST=${API_HOST}
      - API_AUTH_TOKEN=${API_AUTH_TOKEN}
      - RECALL_TOP_K=${RECALL_TOP_K}
      - IMPORTANCE_BOOST=${IMPORTANCE_BOOST}
      - RECENCY_HALF_LIFE_DAYS=${RECENCY_HALF_LIFE_DAYS}
      - MODEL_CACHE=${MODEL_CACHE}
      - LLM_URL=${LLM_URL}
      - LLM_API_KEY=${LLM_API_KEY}
      - LLM_MODEL=${LLM_MODEL}
    depends_on:

        condition: service_healthy

    volumes:
      - modelcache:${MODEL_CACHE}

volumes:
  pgdata:
  modelcache:

FROM python:3.11-slim

ENV PYTHONDONTWRITEBYTECODE=1 \
    PYTHONUNBUFFERED=1

# system deps for psycopg2 and building wheels
RUN apt-get update && apt-get install -y --no-install-recommends \\
    build-essential gcc curl libpq-dev \\
    && rm -rf /var/lib/apt/lists/*

WORKDIR /app

CMD ["uvicorn", "app.main:app", "--host", "0.0.0.0", "--port", "8000"]

# app/schemas.py
schemas = """\
from typing import List, Optional
from pydantic import BaseModel, Field
from datetime import datetime

class EventIn(BaseModel):
    content: str = Field(..., min_length=1, max_length=8192)
    type: str = Field("episodic", pattern="^(episodic|semantic|procedural|identity|social)$")
    tags: List[str] = []
    source: Optional[str] = "mene_portal"
    importance: int = Field(1, ge=0, le=3)  # 0=low,3=critical
    summary: Optional[str] = None

class MemoryOut(BaseModel):
    id: int
    type: str
    content: str
    summary: Optional[str]
    importance: int
    tags: List[str]
    source: Optional[str]
    created_at: datetime
    last_accessed: Optional[datetime]
    recall_count: int
    score: float

class RecallResponse(BaseModel):
    query: str
    results: List[MemoryOut]

class Health(BaseModel):
    status: str
    embed_provider: str
    embed_model: str
"""
open(os.path.join(base, "api/app/schemas.py"), "w").write(schemas)

# app/db.py
db_py = """\
from sqlalchemy import create_engine, text
from sqlalchemy.orm import sessionmaker, declarative_base
import os

POSTGRES_HOST = os.getenv("POSTGRES_HOST", "db")
POSTGRES_PORT = int(os.getenv("POSTGRES_PORT", "5432"))
POSTGRES_DB = os.getenv("POSTGRES_DB", "mene_mem")
POSTGRES_USER = os.getenv("POSTGRES_USER", "mene")
POSTGRES_PASSWORD = os.getenv("POSTGRES_PASSWORD", "change_me")

DATABASE_URL = f"postgresql+psycopg2://{POSTGRES_USER}:{POSTGRES_PASSWORD}@{POSTGRES_HOST}:{POSTGRES_PORT}/{POSTGRES_DB}"

engine = create_engine(DATABASE_URL, pool_pre_ping=True)
SessionLocal = sessionmaker(bind=engine, autoflush=False, autocommit=False)
Base = declarative_base()

def init_db():
    # Ensure pgvector is available
    with engine.connect() as conn:
        conn.execute(text("CREATE EXTENSION IF NOT EXISTS vector"))
        conn.commit()
"""
open(os.path.join(base, "api/app/db.py"), "w").write(db_py)

# app/models.py
models_py = """\
from sqlalchemy import Column, Integer, String, DateTime, Text, ARRAY, Float
from sqlalchemy.dialects.postgresql import VECTOR
from sqlalchemy.sql import func
from sqlalchemy.orm import Mapped, mapped_column
from typing import List, Optional
from .db import Base

class Memory(Base):
    __tablename__ = "memories"
    id: Mapped[int] = mapped_column(Integer, primary_key=True, index=True)
    type: Mapped[str] = mapped_column(String(32), index=True)
    content: Mapped[str] = mapped_column(Text)
    summary: Mapped[Optional[str]] = mapped_column(Text, nullable=True)
    importance: Mapped[int] = mapped_column(Integer, default=1)
    tags: Mapped[Optional[List[str]]] = mapped_column(ARRAY(String), default=list)
    source: Mapped[Optional[str]] = mapped_column(String(64), nullable=True)
    created_at: Mapped = mapped_column(DateTime(timezone=True), server_default=func.now())
    last_accessed: Mapped[Optional] = mapped_column(DateTime(timezone=True), nullable=True)
    recall_count: Mapped[int] = mapped_column(Integer, default=0)
    decay_weight: Mapped[float] = mapped_column(Float, default=1.0)
    embedding = Column(VECTOR(384))  # default; will be verified at runtime
"""
open(os.path.join(base, "api/app/models.py"), "w").write(models_py)

# app/embeddings.py
embeddings_py = """\
import os
import numpy as np
from typing import List

EMBED_PROVIDER = os.getenv("EMBED_PROVIDER", "fastembed").lower()
EMBED_MODEL = os.getenv("EMBED_MODEL", "BAAI/bge-small-en-v1.5")

class Embedder:
    def __init__(self):
        self.provider = EMBED_PROVIDER
        self.model_name = EMBED_MODEL
        if self.provider == "fastembed":
            from fastembed import TextEmbedding
            # cache to MODEL_CACHE
            cache = os.getenv("MODEL_CACHE", os.path.expanduser("~/.cache/mene_ltm"))
            os.makedirs(cache, exist_ok=True)
            self.model = TextEmbedding(model_name=self.model_name, cache_dir=cache)
            # infer dim
            self._dim = len(list(self.model.embed(["dim_probe"]))[0])
        elif self.provider == "openai":
            import httpx
            self.http = httpx.Client(base_url=os.getenv("OPENAI_BASE_URL", "https://api.openai.com/v1"),
                                     headers={
                                         "Authorization": f"Bearer {os.getenv('OPENAI_API_KEY','')}"
                                     })
            self._dim = int(os.getenv("OPENAI_EMBED_DIM", "1536"))
        else:
            raise ValueError(f"Unknown EMBED_PROVIDER: {self.provider}")

    @property
    def dim(self) -> int:
        return self._dim

    def embed(self, texts: List[str]) -> np.ndarray:
        if self.provider == "fastembed":
            vecs = list(self.model.embed(texts))
            return np.array(vecs, dtype="float32")
        else:
            # OpenAI-compatible
            payload = {"input": texts, "model": self.model_name}
            resp = self.http.post("/embeddings", json=payload, timeout=60)
            resp.raise_for_status()
            data = resp.json()["data"]
            return np.array([d["embedding"] for d in data], dtype="float32")
"""
open(os.path.join(base, "api/app/embeddings.py"), "w").write(embeddings_py)

# app/utils.py
utils_py = """\
import math
from datetime import datetime, timezone

def time_decay(created_at, half_life_days: float) -> float:
    if not created_at:
        return 1.0
    now = datetime.now(timezone.utc)
    dt = (now - created_at).total_seconds()
    half_life = half_life_days * 86400.0
    if half_life <= 0:
        return 1.0
    # exponential decay: 0.5^(t/half_life)
    return math.pow(0.5, dt / half_life)
"""
open(os.path.join(base, "api/app/utils.py"), "w").write(utils_py)

# app/crud.py
crud_py = """\
from sqlalchemy.orm import Session
from sqlalchemy import text
from typing import List, Tuple
from .models import Memory
from .embeddings import Embedder
from .utils import time_decay
import os

RECALL_TOP_K = int(os.getenv("RECALL_TOP_K","15"))
IMPORTANCE_BOOST = float(os.getenv("IMPORTANCE_BOOST","0.2"))
RECENCY_HALF_LIFE_DAYS = float(os.getenv("RECENCY_HALF_LIFE_DAYS","14"))

def ensure_vector_dim(db: Session, dim: int):
    # If column exists with different dim, recreate would be needed; we assume 384 default matches
    # This keeps it simple. Advanced migration is out-of-scope here.
    pass

def create_memory(db: Session, embedder: Embedder, content: str, type_: str, tags: list, source: str, importance: int, summary: str|None):
    vec = embedder.embed([content])[0].tolist()
    mem = Memory(
        type=type_,
        content=content,
        summary=summary,
        importance=importance,
        tags=tags or [],
        source=source,
        embedding=vec
    )
    db.add(mem)
    db.commit()
    db.refresh(mem)
    return mem

def recall(db: Session, embedder: Embedder, query: str, tags: list[str]|None=None) -> List[Tuple[Memory, float]]:
    qvec = embedder.embed([query])[0]
    # Base vector search using pgvector cosine distance (<=>). Lower is closer; convert to similarity.
    tag_filter = ""
    if tags:
        tag_filter = "AND tags && :tags"
    sql = text(f\"\"\"
        SELECT id, type, content, summary, importance, tags, source, created_at, last_accessed, recall_count, decay_weight,
               1.0 - (embedding <=> :qvec) AS sim
        FROM memories
        WHERE embedding IS NOT NULL {tag_filter}
        ORDER BY embedding <=> :qvec
        LIMIT :k
    \"\"\")
    rows = db.execute(sql, {"qvec": qvec.tolist(), "k": RECALL_TOP_K, "tags": tags}).mappings().all()

    results = []
    for r in rows:
        # recency decay
        rec = time_decay(r["created_at"], RECENCY_HALF_LIFE_DAYS)
        imp = (r["importance"] or 0) * IMPORTANCE_BOOST
        # tag bonus if provided
        tag_bonus = 0.15 if (tags and r["tags"]) else 0.0
        score = max(0.0, r["sim"]) * 0.7 + imp + rec * 0.15 + tag_bonus
        results.append((r, score))

    # sort by combined score desc
    results.sort(key=lambda tup: tup[1], reverse=True)
    return results

def reinforce(db: Session, mem_id: int, bonus: float=0.1):
    mem = db.query(Memory).get(mem_id)
    if not mem:
        return None
    mem.recall_count = (mem.recall_count or 0) + 1
    mem.decay_weight = (mem.decay_weight or 1.0) * (1.0 + bonus)
    db.commit()
    db.refresh(mem)
    return mem

def forget(db: Session, mem_id: int):
    mem = db.query(Memory).get(mem_id)
    if not mem:
        return None
    db.delete(mem)
    db.commit()
    return True
"""
open(os.path.join(base, "api/app/crud.py"), "w").write(crud_py)

# app/consolidation.py
consolidation_py = """\
import os, httpx
from typing import Optional

LLM_URL = os.getenv("LLM_URL","").strip()
LLM_MODEL = os.getenv("LLM_MODEL","gpt-4o-mini")
LLM_API_KEY = os.getenv("LLM_API_KEY","").strip()

def summarize(text: str, hint: str|None=None) -> Optional[str]:
    if not LLM_URL or not LLM_API_KEY:
        # fallback: crude compression
        txt = text.strip()
        if len(txt) <= 512:
            return txt
        return txt[:480] + "â€¦"
    # OpenAI-compatible chat completions minimal
    headers = {"Authorization": f"Bearer {LLM_API_KEY}"}
    payload = {
        "model": LLM_MODEL,
        "messages": [
            {"role":"system","content":"Summarize crisply in <=80 words; include key facts, numbers, names, dates."},
            {"role":"user","content": text if not hint else f"Hint: {hint}\\n\\nText:\\n{text}"}
        ],
        "temperature": 0.2,
        "max_tokens": 180
    }
    with httpx.Client() as http:
        r = http.post(LLM_URL.rstrip('/') + "/chat/completions", json=payload, headers=headers, timeout=60)
        r.raise_for_status()
        return r.json()["choices"][0]["message"]["content"].strip()
"""
open(os.path.join(base, "api/app/consolidation.py"), "w").write(consolidation_py)

# app/main.py
main_py = """\
import os
from fastapi import FastAPI, Depends, HTTPException, Header
from fastapi.middleware.cors import CORSMiddleware
from sqlalchemy.orm import Session
from typing import Optional, List
from .db import SessionLocal, Base, engine, init_db
from .models import Memory
from .schemas import EventIn, RecallResponse, MemoryOut, Health
from .embeddings import Embedder
from . import crud
from .consolidation import summarize
from datetime import datetime, timezone

AUTH_TOKEN = os.getenv("API_AUTH_TOKEN","dev_token_change_me")

app = FastAPI(title="Mene Long-Term Memory (M-LTM)")

app.add_middleware(
    CORSMiddleware,
    allow_origins=["*"], allow_credentials=True, allow_methods=["*"], allow_headers=["*"],
)

# Global singleton-ish
EMBEDDER = Embedder()

def get_db():
    db = SessionLocal()
    try:
        yield db
    finally:
        db.close()

@app.on_event("startup")
def on_start():
    init_db()
    Base.metadata.create_all(bind=engine)

def require_auth(authorization: Optional[str] = Header(None)):
    if not authorization or not authorization.startswith("Bearer "):
        raise HTTPException(status_code=401, detail="Missing bearer token")
    token = authorization.split(" ",1)[1].strip()
    if token != AUTH_TOKEN:
        raise HTTPException(status_code=403, detail="Invalid token")

@app.get("/health", response_model=Health)
def health(_: None = Depends(require_auth)):
    return Health(status="ok", embed_provider=EMBEDDER.provider, embed_model=EMBEDDER.model_name)

@app.post("/event")
def write_event(event: EventIn, _: None = Depends(require_auth), db: Session = Depends(get_db)):
    mem = crud.create_memory(
        db=db,
        embedder=EMBEDDER,
        content=event.content,
        type_=event.type,
        tags=event.tags,
        source=event.source or "mene_portal",
        importance=event.importance,
        summary=event.summary or summarize(event.content)
    )
    return {"id": mem.id, "summary": mem.summary}

@app.get("/recall", response_model=RecallResponse)
def recall(q: str, tags: Optional[str] = None, _: None = Depends(require_auth), db: Session = Depends(get_db)):
    tag_list = [t.strip() for t in tags.split(",")] if tags else None
    rows = crud.recall(db, EMBEDDER, q, tag_list)
    out: List[MemoryOut] = []
    for r, score in rows:
        # Update last_accessed and recall_count
        mem = db.query(Memory).get(r["id"])
        mem.last_accessed = datetime.now(timezone.utc)
        mem.recall_count = (mem.recall_count or 0) + 1
        db.commit()
        out.append(MemoryOut(
            id=r["id"], type=r["type"], content=r["content"], summary=r["summary"],
            importance=r["importance"], tags=r["tags"] or [], source=r["source"],
            created_at=r["created_at"], last_accessed=r["last_accessed"],
            recall_count=r["recall_count"], score=round(score,4)
        ))
    return RecallResponse(query=q, results=out)

@app.post("/reinforce/{mem_id}")
def reinforce(mem_id: int, _: None = Depends(require_auth), db: Session = Depends(get_db)):
    mem = crud.reinforce(db, mem_id)
    if not mem:
        raise HTTPException(status_code=404, detail="not found")
    return {"ok": True, "id": mem.id, "decay_weight": mem.decay_weight, "recall_count": mem.recall_count}

@app.post("/forget/{mem_id}")
def forget(mem_id: int, _: None = Depends(require_auth), db: Session = Depends(get_db)):
    ok = crud.forget(db, mem_id)
    if not ok:
        raise HTTPException(status_code=404, detail="not found")
    return {"ok": True}

@app.get("/dump")
def dump(_: None = Depends(require_auth), db: Session = Depends(get_db)):
    rows = db.query(Memory).order_by(Memory.created_at.desc()).all()
    return {
        "count": len(rows),
        "items": [
            {
                "id": m.id, "type": m.type, "content": m.content, "summary": m.summary,
                "importance": m.importance, "tags": m.tags or [], "source": m.source,
                "created_at": m.created_at, "last_accessed": m.last_accessed, "recall_count": m.recall_count
            } for m in rows
        ]
    }
"""
open(os.path.join(base, "api/app/main.py"), "w").write(main_py)

# CLI script
cli = """\
#!/usr/bin/env python3
import argparse, os, json, requests

API = os.getenv("MENE_MEM_API", "http://localhost:8000")
TOKEN = os.getenv("MENE_MEM_TOKEN", "dev_token_change_me")
HEAD = {"Authorization": f"Bearer {TOKEN}"}

def post_event(args):
    payload = {
        "content": args.content,
        "type": args.type,
        "tags": [t.strip() for t in (args.tags or "").split(",") if t.strip()],
        "source": args.source,
        "importance": args.importance
    }
    r = requests.post(f"{API}/event", headers=HEAD, json=payload, timeout=30)
    print(r.status_code, json.dumps(r.json(), indent=2))

def recall(args):
    r = requests.get(f"{API}/recall", headers=HEAD, params={"q": args.query, "tags": args.tags or ""}, timeout=30)
    print(r.status_code, json.dumps(r.json(), indent=2))

def main():
    p = argparse.ArgumentParser(description="Mene LTM CLI")
    sub = p.add_subparsers(dest="cmd")

    p1 = sub.add_parser("event", help="Write memory event")
    p1.add_argument("-c","--content", required=True)
    p1.add_argument("-t","--type", default="episodic")
    p1.add_argument("--tags", default="")
    p1.add_argument("-s","--source", default="cli")
    p1.add_argument("-i","--importance", type=int, default=1)
    p1.set_defaults(func=post_event)

    p2 = sub.add_parser("recall", help="Recall")
    p2.add_argument("-q","--query", required=True)
    p2.add_argument("--tags", default="")
    p2.set_defaults(func=recall)

    args = p.parse_args()
    if not args.cmd:
        p.print_help()
        return
    args.func(args)

if __name__ == "__main__":
    main()
"""
open(os.path.join(base, "cli/mene_mem.py"), "w").write(cli)
os.chmod(os.path.join(base, "cli/mene_mem.py"), 0o755)

# What â€œrealâ€ long-term memory looks like
**Shape:** a persistent store of *salient* facts/events about you, your projects and routines, not just chat logs. Split it like this:
- **Episodic** (what happened, when/where).
- **Semantic** (facts you care about; tools, creds, settings).
- **Procedural** (how to do recurring tasks).
- **Identity/Social** (your prefs; whoâ€™s who; roles).
- Each item has: `content`, `summary`, `type`, `tags`, `importance (0â€“3)`, `source`, `created_at`, `last_accessed`, `embedding`.

**Lifecycle (human-style):**  
_perceive â†’ select â†’ encode â†’ store â†’ consolidate â†’ recall â†’ reinforce/forget_.  
The trick is **salience gating** (donâ€™t store everything) and **consolidation** (compress/merge over time). Todayâ€™s best open libraries do variants of this, but theyâ€™re still frameworks, not finished memory (e.g., LangGraph/LangChain/LlamaIndex long-term memory patterns; MemGPT research). îˆ€citeîˆ‚turn0search5îˆ‚turn0search15îˆ‚turn0search10îˆ‚turn0search2îˆ‚turn0search6îˆ‚turn0search22îˆ

**Recall that actually helps the agent:**
- **Hybrid score** = vector similarity (what itâ€™s about) Ã— **recency decay** (freshness) Ã— **importance boost** (what you told me matters) + **tag/goal match** (context).  
- **Hook recall before planning**: every agent call starts with `recall(goal/topic)` to pull the right 5â€“15 nuggets, then writes back new/updated items after the turn.

**What makes it work (no smoke)**  
- **Persistent DB + vector index** (Postgres + pgvector = reliable + simple deploy). îˆ€citeîˆ‚turn0search3îˆ‚turn0search7îˆ‚turn0search12îˆ‚turn0search16îˆ  
- **Light, fast embeddings** you can run on CPU (e.g., **BAAI/bge-small-en-v1.5**, 384-dim). îˆ€citeîˆ‚turn0search4îˆ‚turn0search14îˆ‚turn0search21îˆ  
- **Clear write policy** (only salient) + **scheduled consolidation** (summarise & merge facts).  
- **Explicit reinforcement/forgetting** buttons (you control drift).  
- **Interface**: tiny REST API any agent can call (Portal, phone, scripts).

Whatâ€™s inside:
- **FastAPI service** (`/event`, `/recall`, `/reinforce/{id}`, `/forget/{id}`, `/dump`).
- **Postgres + pgvector** via `docker-compose`.
- **Local embeddings** by default via **FastEmbed** (CPU). OpenAI-compatible option wired too. îˆ€citeîˆ‚turn0search8îˆ‚turn0search20îˆ

## Install (5 min)
1) Unzip somewhere (PC/WSL or Linux).  
2) Copy env + set secrets:
```bash
cd mene-ltm
cp .env.example .env
# edit .env -> set POSTGRES_PASSWORD and API_AUTH_TOKEN
```
3) Bring it up:
```bash
docker compose up -d --build
```
4) Health check:
```bash
curl -H "Authorization: Bearer YOUR_TOKEN" http://localhost:8000/health
```

## Use it
Write a salient memory:
```bash
curl -X POST http://localhost:8000/event \
  -H "Authorization: Bearer YOUR_TOKEN" \
  -H "Content-Type: application/json" \
  -d '{
    "content": "Booked kitesurf repair in Durban North Friday 10:00",
    "type": "episodic",
    "tags": ["kitesurf","repair","schedule"],
    "source": "mene_portal",
    "importance": 2
  }'
```

Recall before planning:
```bash
curl "http://localhost:8000/recall?q=kitesurf repair" \
  -H "Authorization: Bearer YOUR_TOKEN"
```

Reinforce or forget:
```bash
curl -X POST -H "Authorization: Bearer YOUR_TOKEN" http://localhost:8000/reinforce/123
curl -X POST -H "Authorization: Bearer YOUR_TOKEN" http://localhost:8000/forget/123
```

## Wire it into Meneâ€™ Portal (pattern)
**Before** the LLM plans a response:
- Call `/recall?q=<user goal or current topic>&tags=<project>` â†’ take top 10 summaries into system/context.
**After** the turn:
- If new commitments/facts emerged, `POST /event` with `importance` and `tags`.

Python sketch:
```python
import requests, os

API=os.getenv("MENE_MEM_API","http://localhost:8000")
TOK=os.getenv("MENE_MEM_TOKEN")

def recall(topic, tags=None):
    r = requests.get(f"{API}/recall", params={"q": topic, "tags": ",".join(tags or [])},
                     headers={"Authorization": f"Bearer {TOK}"}, timeout=20)
    r.raise_for_status()
    return [m["summary"] or m["content"] for m in r.json()["results"]]

def write_event(content, type="episodic", tags=None, importance=1, source="mene_portal"):
    payload = {"content": content, "type": type, "tags": tags or [], "importance": importance, "source": source}
    requests.post(f"{API}/event", json=payload, headers={"Authorization": f"Bearer {TOK}"}, timeout=20).raise_for_status()
```

Android quick capture (Termux shortcut):
```bash
mem() {
  curl -s -X POST "$MENE_MEM_API/event" \
    -H "Authorization: Bearer $MENE_MEM_TOKEN" \
    -H "Content-Type: application/json" \
    -d "{\"content\":\"$*\",\"type\":\"episodic\",\"tags\":[\"mobile\",\"note\"],\"importance\":1}"
}
# usage: mem Pick up parcel from PEP 16:30
```

# Why this beats â€œchat historyâ€
- **Persistent store** with **pgvector** gives exact/ANN nearest-neighbour search plus proper SQL joins and backups. îˆ€citeîˆ‚turn0search3îˆ  
- **FastEmbed + BGE-small** is CPU-friendly and 384-dim (we set the column accordingly). îˆ€citeîˆ‚turn0search20îˆ‚turn0search14îˆ  
- **Scoring** balances meaning (similarity), time (decay), and your intent (importance/tags).  
- **Consolidation** endpoint is wired: if you give me an LLM URL, it summarises; otherwise it compresses locally.

Audit of AI Assistant Stack & TTS Engine Plan

Overview & Context

Robâ€™s Meneâ€™ Assist AI ecosystem is a multi-component system combining local and cloud AI services. It includes retrieval-augmented memory stores, multiple AI agent personas (Meneâ€™, Bonny, â€œGrumpy Steveâ€, etc.), and voice input/output features. The goal is to streamline this into a scalable assistant interface (ideally a cross-device Progressive Web App or PWA) with robust text-to-speech (TTS), speech-to-text (STT), and memory integration. This report audits the current components â€“ identifying which pieces are functional, redundant, or broken â€“ and recommends an integrated stack design. It also evaluates building a lightweight TTS engine (using OpenVoice or alternatives) for clarity and natural prosody, and outlines PWA implementation options. A summary stack integration map and key risks are included.

1. Text-to-Speech Engine Options

Rob requires a high-quality TTS voice that is clear, natural in prosody, and fits his local stack. The preferred deployment is a browser-based PWA (optionally packaged to Android/Windows), meaning the TTS solution should be accessible via web API or runnable on local hardware.

OpenVoice (MyShell AI) â€“ Instant Voice Cloning Model
- Capabilities: OpenVoice (v1 & v2) can clone a speakerâ€™s voice from a short audio sample, producing speech in multiple languages and accents with high fidelity[1]. It offers granular control over voice style (emotion, accent, rhythm, intonation) and even cross-lingual cloning (zero-shot)[1]. The newer V2 (released Apr 2024) further improves audio quality and has native multi-lingual support (English, Spanish, French, Chinese, Japanese, Korean)[2]. Both V1 and V2 are MIT-licensed (open-source), free for commercial use[3], making this an attractive base for a custom TTS engine. - Usage & Integration: OpenVoice is implemented in Python (with ~34k GitHub stars, indicating maturity). It likely requires a GPU for real-time performance, though CPU usage is possible with trade-offs. To use it in the PWA, one could run an OpenVoice service on a server or powerful local machine and expose a REST API endpoint. For example, a request would send text (and a reference voice embedding or ID) to the server and return an audio file or stream. Because OpenVoice is an instant cloning model, Rob could provide a one-time reference recording of the desired voice (e.g. his own voice or a personaâ€™s voice) to create a voice profile, then reuse that for all TTS outputs. This avoids reliance on third-party services while delivering very natural speech. - Adaptation Needed? Minimal. OpenVoice can be used largely as-is, but integration code is needed to serve it via an API. For instance, wrapping the OpenVoice model in a lightweight Flask/FastAPI web service that the PWA can call. Additional adaptation might include optimizing the model for lower resource usage if itâ€™s too heavy â€“ e.g. using the smaller V1 model if V2 is too slow, or running on GPU in the cloud when available. Given that OpenVoice is based on advanced TTS architectures (VITS variants), the quality should meet Robâ€™s prosody clarity needs, but testing is needed to ensure it runs smoothly on his hardware or VM.

Piper (Rhasspy) â€“ Current LocalAI Default
- Capabilities: Piper is a fast, lightweight TTS engine using pre-trained voices (ONNX models). In the current LocalAI configuration, the default TTS is Piper with an en-US voice (â€œAmyâ€). Piper voices are intelligible and work offline on CPU, but the speech is somewhat robotic or generic compared to neural-cloning solutions. Prosody is acceptable but not highly expressive. - Integration: Piper is already integrated in LocalAIâ€™s pipeline (accessible via a local /tts API). Itâ€™s very easy to use and efficient, making it a good fallback for low-resource devices. However, it does not offer custom voice cloning â€“ you are limited to the provided voices (though there might be some UK or SA accent voices available in Rhasspyâ€™s library that could partially match Robâ€™s preferences). - Assessment: Piper is functional and requires no additional work to use via LocalAI, but it may not meet the â€œnatural prosodyâ€ bar if Rob expects a more life-like or South African-accented voice. Itâ€™s a practical short-term solution (especially if running on modest hardware), but for a truly engaging assistant voice, a more advanced engine (OpenVoice or similar) is preferable.

Other TTS Alternatives:
- ElevenLabs API: A cloud TTS with excellent quality and voice cloning. Rob has used ElevenLabs for â€œBonnyâ€™s voiceâ€ in the past and even considered upgrading his subscription to clone voices from real recordings (per notes). It offers very natural speech. However, it is a paid external service and using it continuously for an â€œalways-onâ€ assistant could incur significant cost and dependency on internet. Since Rob prioritizes local autonomy and privacy, relying on ElevenLabs long-term goes against the local-first philosophy (ElevenLabs was referenced as part of the voice stack blueprint[4], but likely as an interim solution). Itâ€™s best kept as a backup or for specific use (e.g. one-time voice creation that can then be ported to a local model). - Mozilla TTS / Coqui: Open-source TTS frameworks from Mozilla/Coqui were mentioned as alternatives[4]. These include models like Tacotron2 or VITS that one can fine-tune. They can produce natural voices if trained on a target voice, but training/fine-tuning is non-trivial and requires a dataset of the target voice. Out-of-the-box, Coqui provides some pre-trained voices and multi-speaker models. Quality can be good, though not necessarily at the level of ElevenLabs or OpenVoiceâ€™s cloning. Using Coqui would entail either adopting an existing model that fits Robâ€™s needs or investing time to train a custom model (which is likely beyond scope and unnecessary given OpenVoiceâ€™s availability). - Bark by Suno: Robâ€™s GitHub includes a fork of â€œBarkâ€, another transformer-based TTS model. Bark generates expressive speech and can even produce nonverbal sounds. It doesnâ€™t require a reference voice, but it has a stochastic output â€“ the voice can vary and itâ€™s hard to get a consistent specific persona voice without fine-tuning. Bark is also heavy (generally runs on GPU for reasonable speed) and may produce unpredictable prosody. Itâ€™s powerful, but if voice consistency and clarity are top priorities, OpenVoice or ElevenLabs are more deterministic choices.

TTS Recommendation: Integrate OpenVoice as the core TTS engine, with Piper or Coqui as a fallback. OpenVoice aligns with Robâ€™s requirements: itâ€™s self-hosted, high-quality, and supports accent/emotion control[1]. The voice cloning ability means Rob can have a unique voice for Meneâ€™ (for example, cloning a South African English speaker to get that Durban tone he likes). To implement: set up a service using OpenVoice (possibly on the Windows 11 workstation with GPU acceleration). The PWA can then send text to this service and stream or download the generated audio. If OpenVoice is found to be too resource-intensive for some devices, Piper can be used when running purely offline on a low-power device, albeit with reduced naturalness. In the long run, as hardware improves, OpenVoice can even be packaged into a local app or WASM for on-device use â€“ but initially, a lightweight client (browser) + heavy server model is the pragmatic approach.

Why not use OpenVoice â€œas-isâ€? â€“ The core model is ready to use, but we need to â€œproductizeâ€ it: writing some glue code for API hosting, and possibly creating a small library of voice encodings (for example, an encoding for â€œMeneâ€™s voiceâ€ derived from a sample). This is a manageable adaptation. Given OpenVoiceâ€™s popularity and MIT license, community tools or wrappers may already exist (for instance, some have integrated OpenVoice into UIs or automation flows). We should also monitor its resource usage; if needed, limit the audio sample rate or use V1 model to save compute. Overall, OpenVoice is a suitable base, requiring only moderate integration work â€“ a worthwhile trade-off for the jump in voice quality and persona realism.

2. RAG Pipeline & Memory Integration Audit

Retrieval-Augmented Generation (RAG) is central to giving Meneâ€™ long-term memory and context. Robâ€™s setup includes tools like AnythingLLM and ChromaDB for document embeddings, and possibly frameworks like LlamaIndex (as evidenced by a prior attempt to implement a RAG system using LlamaIndex + Chroma). The question is whether these components are properly wired into the assistantâ€™s dialogue and where gaps exist.

Current Memory Components:
- AnythingLLM: This is a document management and vector store system that Rob downloaded (noted ~300MB). It likely provides an interface to ingest documents and create embeddings (using ChromaDB under the hood) and possibly an API to query them. Robâ€™s F:\AI_RAM drive and the integration with OpenWebUI indicate that AnythingLLM is hosting a local knowledge base[5]. It is a functional piece (the software runs), but integration is incomplete: thereâ€™s no evidence that the main assistant (Meneâ€™) automatically queries AnythingLLM during conversation yet. In other words, the data is there, but the agent isnâ€™t â€œrememberingâ€ to use it without explicit prompting. - OpenWebUI (Multi-Agent Chat UI): OpenWebUI is cited as a RAG-ready multi-agent chat interface[6]. It likely allows connecting a vector database or knowledge base to supplement the LLMâ€™s answers. If properly set up, OpenWebUI could retrieve context from AnythingLLMâ€™s data when questions are asked. However, this requires configuration â€“ e.g. â€œdocumentsâ€ mode or enabling retrieval plugins. Itâ€™s unclear if this is fully operational. The Research Gaps explicitly mention the need to unify the UI between AnythingLLM and OpenWebUI and voice[7], suggesting that as of now these pieces are somewhat siloed (redundant interfaces). OpenWebUI can host multiple agent personas (e.g. Bonny, Steve) and orchestrate them, but whether itâ€™s currently pulling from the vector memory is uncertain. This likely needs validation and possibly custom integration code (perhaps using LlamaIndex or LlamaIndexâ€™s integration to OpenAI API). - LlamaIndex / Custom RAG Code: In a past chat, Meneâ€™ attempted to produce a rag_system.py using LlamaIndex (GPT Index) with ChromaDB as the vector store. That indicates at least a plan or prototype code exists to wire up embeddings and retrieval in a single script. If that code was saved and run, it could serve as a back-end memory service. The code was described as â€œFull Minimal RAG System â€“ LlamaIndex + ChromaDB â€“ All-in-one file with setup, ingestion, and queryâ€ (with Docker/Notebook options) to be run on the GCP VM. The status of this is unclear: it might be partially written in a notebook or just as text in the chat logs. We should treat it as potentially available but not deployed. If not actually deployed, this is a broken/unrealized component â€“ a plan that needs finishing. If it was deployed, it overlaps with AnythingLLMâ€™s functionality (embedding storage and querying), possibly duplicating effort. - ChromaDB Vector Store: ChromaDB itself is a solid choice for vector database. The blueprint explicitly says â€œStore embeddings in ChromaDB (via AnythingLLM or OpenWebUI)â€[8]. So the architecture calls for Chroma as the memory index, regardless of which front-end uses it. Itâ€™s likely functional (Chroma is embedded in AnythingLLM). Functional vs Broken: Storing vectors works â€“ Rob has uploaded many personal documents and chat logs into the system. The missing piece is automated retrieval integration â€“ i.e. when the user asks a question, does Meneâ€™ automatically fetch relevant context chunks from Chroma to include in the prompt? Currently, this integration appears broken or not yet built. The frustration Rob expressed (â€œcode me a RAG systemâ€¦ let me watch you put five linesâ€¦â€) highlights that previous promises of persistent memory were not delivered, meaning the assistant was not successfully leveraging the vector store in conversations.

Integration with Agents:
- Master Agent Orchestration: The vision is that Meneâ€™ acts as a master orchestrator agent that can call tools or delegate to sub-agents. There are personas (Bonny for research, Steve for critical reasoning) meant to be invoked for specific tasks[9]. Right now, this multi-agent system is likely not fully functional â€“ itâ€™s outlined in design, but not implemented in code. The OpenWebUI can host multiple characters in one interface (that part is functional as a UI), but programming the logic for â€œwhen to hand off to Bonny or Steveâ€ is still a work in progress. This is more of an enhancement than a broken component; it simply hasnâ€™t been built out yet. - LiveKit Agent Pipeline: Separately, thereâ€™s the LiveKit AgentSession pipeline (from the agent-starter-python template[10][11]). This is functional for voice calls (it sets up STT, LLM, TTS in a streaming session) and includes evaluation tests. However, itâ€™s a standalone pipeline not connected to the long-term memory. It uses GPT-4 (or gpt-4o-mini in code) for responses and doesnâ€™t pull in external knowledge. In fact, it explicitly instructs the agent with a short system prompt and has no mention of vector DB usage[12]. Thus, while the LiveKit agent runs, it lacks RAG integration and operates more like a voice-enabled ChatGPT. Given Robâ€™s emphasis on robust memory, this pipeline in its base form is insufficient. Itâ€™s a functional component that duplicates capabilities available elsewhere (voice chat) but doesnâ€™t solve the memory problem. It may be partly redundant if we move to a unified PWA approach (because LiveKit was one way to get voice chat working quickly, but a custom web app could handle voice too without that overhead). LiveKitâ€™s strength is real-time audio streaming in a call scenario â€“ if Rob needs phone-call style interactions, itâ€™s useful. If not, it might be overkill.

Findings â€“ Functional vs Redundant vs Broken:
- Functional: The raw building blocks â€“ ChromaDB (vector store), embedding models (from AnythingLLM), document UIs (AnythingLLM web, OpenWebUI chat), and LLM access (OpenAI API, local LLaMA via LM Studio) â€“ are available and working individually. The voice input (Whisper) and voice output (Cartesia TTS or ElevenLabs via API) have been proven in at least one context. The LocalAI service is presumably functional (it provides REST endpoints to run local models and multimodal tasks). So, the â€œpieces on the tableâ€ are functional. - Redundant: There is clear overlap in interfaces: running both AnythingLLM and OpenWebUI with separate UIs could be redundant â€“ both aim to provide chat with memory. Two vector stores arenâ€™t needed; standardizing on Chroma is fine (and it seems Chroma is the one being used by all). Also, Robâ€™s GitHub has multiple â€œassistantâ€ frameworks (LiveKit agent starter, CopilotKit, etc.). Pursuing all in parallel is redundant; itâ€™s better to consolidate to one pipeline. For example, CopilotKit (an open-source agent framework) was noted as something to explore[7]. If CopilotKit was imported, it might duplicate some logic that LiveKit or LocalAI already cover. We should choose one unified framework rather than maintain many. - Another redundant pairing is cloud vs local LLM: Rob has access to Googleâ€™s Vertex AI (Gemini models) and OpenAI, but also local LLaMA 2 via LM Studio[13]. In design, the system can route queries to either cloud or local models depending on context[14]. This is a great feature (â€œhybrid model usageâ€), but if not carefully orchestrated it can be over-engineered. At present, probably only one model is used at a time (e.g. GPT-4 or Gemini for high-stakes queries, LLaMA for offline). The redundancy risk is maintaining two integration paths. However, given the end-goal (a flexible system), this is acceptable and not so much broken as just complex. - Broken/Not Yet Implemented: The actual RAG integration is the critical broken piece. The assistant should be able to recall knowledge from Robâ€™s archives (PDFs, logs, notes) on demand. Currently, this does not happen reliably. We see evidence of this breakage in the â€œTruth Modeâ€ documents â€“ promises were made to implement persistent memory which fell through, causing trust issues. Thus the highest priority is to fix the RAG pipeline â€“ ensure that whenever relevant, the assistant fetches context snippets from Chroma and includes them (with citations or at least with improved factual accuracy) in responses. - Also broken or inconsistent is conversation persistence: the existence of â€œpartial memory dumpsâ€ and manual saving of chat logs suggests the session memory was often lost. A RAG system can alleviate this by storing conversation embeddings or summaries. We should verify if any mechanism now stores conversation history into the vector DB (some frameworks do that by default). If not, implementing that (e.g. store each resolved answer as a doc in Chroma) would help long-term context. - Voice pipeline integration is partially broken in the sense that voice input/output isnâ€™t unified with the main assistant across all platforms. For instance, using voice in OpenWebUI might not be configured (OpenWebUI likely doesnâ€™t have built-in STT/TTS). The LiveKit pipeline had it, but thatâ€™s separate. So for the unified PWA, we need to wire Whisper and TTS together with the assistantâ€™s brain. That needs custom work (though straightforward using available libraries or the LocalAI endpoints).

Summary: The knowledge vault and agents exist, but they act in silos. The audit shows a need to integrate them: connect the vector memory to the conversation loop, connect the voice interface to the chat UI, and consolidate duplicative frameworks. No major component is completely defunct â€“ but many are underutilized or need finishing touches. Next, we provide recommendations to achieve a unified stack that meets Robâ€™s goals.

3. Build Recommendations for a Unified Assistant

Based on the audit, hereâ€™s how to proceed with building the PWA-based assistant and integrating the stack:

3.1 Unify the AI Stack (Local + Cloud)

Use LocalAI as the central runtime for the assistantâ€™s AI tasks, extending it where needed. LocalAI is a self-hosted OpenAI-compatible API that already supports text completions, embeddings, image generation, and more on local models. It also has a plugin-like YAML pipeline system for tasks (as seen with Piper TTS, Whisper STT, etc.). By standardizing on LocalAI, Rob can avoid juggling multiple backends at runtime.

Integrate LLMs: LocalAI can run local LLMs (like LLaMA 2 via GGML or other formats) and possibly proxy to cloud models if configured. One idea: set up LocalAI so that the default /v1/chat/completions uses a local model for quick responses, but for certain queries or when a special flag is set, it routes to a cloud model (Gemini via Vertex or OpenAI GPT) for a more powerful answer. This way, the PWA can always call one endpoint (LocalAI), and LocalAI decides which model to use. This would implement the â€œhybrid modelâ€ approach noted in design[14]. If LocalAI doesnâ€™t natively support routing, a simple custom API layer can do this logic.

Integrate Vector DB (Memory): Instead of using AnythingLLMâ€™s separate API, use LocalAIâ€™s embedding endpoint (or a small Python service) to hook into ChromaDB. For example, LocalAI can generate embeddings for a query and we can manually query Chroma for similar docs, then append them to the prompt. We might have to implement a custom retriever component: when the PWA sends a user query, perform: (1) embedding generation (using a model like text-embedding-ada or a local equivalent), (2) similarity search in Chroma, (3) compose a system or context message with the top results, (4) then call the LLM. This logic can reside server-side (for speed and to keep API simple for the PWA). It could be a lightweight Python script or even integrated into LocalAI if it supports function calling or tools. (Since LocalAI is extensible, one could add a â€œretrievalâ€ tool that the model can call, though that might complicate prompts.)

Agent Orchestration: Maintain the idea of a master assistant (Meneâ€™) that can delegate, but implement this pragmatically after the core is working. Initially, we can hardcode some behaviors: e.g., if query is research-oriented, use the â€œBonnyâ€ persona or even just a different system prompt style; if query needs critical reasoning, have â€œSteveâ€ critique the answer (perhaps by doing two passes: one answer, then an evaluation). This can be done with prompt engineering inside the single model. A more advanced multi-agent debate (with separate models or instances) could be attempted later, but it might be overkill. The PWA can provide a UI to show â€œBonnyâ€™s answerâ€ vs â€œSteveâ€™s commentsâ€ if we go that route, but the first goal is to get one good answer with retrieval working.

Frontend (PWA) Simplification: The PWA should not need to handle multiple backends or complex logic. Its role is to provide an interface (text or voice input, and output playback). All heavy lifting (LLM, retrieval, etc.) should happen on the server side (the Windows 11 machine or cloud VM where LocalAI and Chroma run). This keeps the PWA lightweight and device-agnostic.

By unifying around LocalAI + Chroma, we eliminate redundancy: one service handles model inference, one DB handles memory. We can retire the separate AnythingLLM UI (though we might keep it around for admin â€“ e.g., to easily upload new documents and embed them). We also wonâ€™t need LiveKit for the web scenario, since we can manage audio in the PWA directly (more on that below).

3.2 Voice Stack Integration

To achieve an end-to-end voice assistant in the PWA, we need STT (speech-to-text) and TTS integrated with the conversation loop:

Speech Input (STT): Use OpenAI Whisper model (large-v2 or medium) locally for transcription. Whisper is open-source and can run on Windows (possibly via the GPU for faster processing). There are also WebAssembly builds of Whisper; however, running it in the browser for long utterances may be too slow. A better approach is to record audio in the PWA (using the Web Audio API) and send the audio file (or stream) to the server. On the server, either:

Call LocalAIâ€™s STT endpoint â€“ The aio/speech-to-text.yaml in LocalAI likely uses the Whisper model or similar. If configured, one could do POST /stt with the audio and get text back.

Standalone Whisper server: Alternatively, run a small server with Faster-Whisper or Whisper.cpp to handle transcription. This could be triggered by the PWA uploading the audio.

Accuracy vs Censorship: Rob noted voice input issues with Windows Speech (Voice Access vs Voice Typing). Whisper would bypass those issues entirely, since itâ€™s not censored and is quite accurate for accented English. This aligns with Robâ€™s needs for uncensored, robust STT.

Once transcribed, the text input goes into the normal assistant pipeline (with memory retrieval then LLM).

Speech Output (TTS): As discussed, implement an OpenVoice TTS service on the server. The PWA would fetch the synthesized audio. We should choose a common audio format (e.g. WAV or OGG). For web, streaming an OGG file or an MP3 might be easiest for quick playback. Initially, we can generate the whole sentence audio and let the PWA play it once ready (a short delay). In future, we could attempt streaming chunked audio for longer answers (thatâ€™s more complex and likely not needed right away).

For responsiveness, if using a heavy model like OpenVoice, consider a two-tier approach: for short responses, generate with OpenVoice live. For very long responses, it might be faster to generate a quick interim speech (or even use a fast TTS to summarize) â€“ but this is probably unnecessary if the assistant is encouraged to be concise.

Ensure the voice persona is consistent: once we clone a voice for Meneâ€™, use that same voice parameters each time so the user feels itâ€™s the â€œsame personâ€ speaking. Given OpenVoiceâ€™s strength, we can get a South African English male voice clone, aligning with the persona described in Meneâ€™s profile (Durban inflection, informal tone).

Voice Control UI: The PWA should have a simple microphone button to start recording and a way to play the response audio. Thankfully, implementing this is straightforward with modern browsers. We must also handle the case of text input (when the user types instead of speaks) â€“ the system should still respond, and optionally still speak the answer (maybe an option â€œmute voiceâ€ could be toggled if user only wants text).

Optional Standalone Packaging: Because Rob wanted optional packaging into an Android APK or Windows EXE: We can use Tauri or Electron for Windows (to wrap the PWA in a native shell). For Android, PWAs can be installed or we can use Bubblewrap/TWA to convert the PWA into an APK that essentially loads the web app. These are secondary steps â€“ first get the web app working in the browser, then add those if needed.

Current Voice Tools Reuse:
- The LiveKit agent code used Deepgram STT and Cartesia TTS â€“ those are cloud services. We likely wonâ€™t reuse those in the new stack (to reduce dependencies). However, one valuable thing to carry over is the audio handling logic from the LiveKit React frontend example. LiveKitâ€™s web example already demonstrates recording audio, streaming it to the server, and playing back audio. We can adapt that code minus the LiveKit-specific parts. Essentially, rather than sending audio to a LiveKit room, we send it to our Whisper endpoint; and rather than receiving audio via LiveKit, we fetch it from our TTS endpoint. By reusing the UI/UX concepts from that example, we accelerate PWA development (it was mentioned that a â€œvoice assistant widget for any websiteâ€ exists[15] â€“ we can draw inspiration from that). - If any of Robâ€™s GitHub voice projects (e.g. the bark repo) included a basic web player UI, that could also be repurposed.

In summary, for voice: Stick to Whisper + OpenVoice for the new build, both self-hosted. Retire the use of Deepgram/Cartesia/ElevenLabs in daily operation (they can serve as backups or for cross-checking quality). This will ensure the entire pipeline (STT -> LLM+Memory -> TTS) runs locally or on Robâ€™s controlled cloud, aligning with privacy and cost goals.

3.3 Progressive Web App Implementation

The PWA will be the user-facing tip of the spear. It should be intuitive and lightweight:

Tech Stack: A simple single-page application using a framework like React or Svelte will work. React with Next.js could be used if we anticipate complex state, but even plain HTML/JS with some libraries could do (since the features are: record audio, send text, display chat bubbles, play audio). There are also starter templates (as LiveKit and others provided) which we can use as baseline. We should ensure itâ€™s a Progressive Web App (meaning it has a manifest, can be installed to homescreen, and possibly works offline for caching transcripts or static assets). Full offline functionality (for the AI responses) is not feasible unless we download models to the device, which isnâ€™t realistic for a phone â€“ so an online connection to the backend is assumed.

UI Design: Following Robâ€™s preferences, the assistant should present responses in a structured way (Markdown formatting, lists when appropriate). The PWA can render Markdown for rich text answers. It can also show source citations (if we include those in answers) to build trust â€“ e.g. showing the user which document a fact came from (the system can provide these since we have the source from Chroma). Additionally, because multiple personas exist (Meneâ€™, Bonny, Steve), the UI might have a way to indicate who is â€œspeakingâ€ â€“ perhaps with an avatar or a label. For now, we can just use one persona (Meneâ€™) and later allow a toggle or automatic persona injection if needed.

Multidevice: PWA inherently works on desktop and mobile browsers. We should test UI responsiveness (microphone access on mobile, audio playback, etc.). On Windows, Chrome or Edge can install the PWA as an app. On Android, Chrome also allows adding to home screen, or we convert to APK if needed. On iOS, PWA installation works via Safari (with some limitations, but basic functionality should be fine as long as we use standard APIs).

Optional Native Enhancements: If certain device integrations are needed (like background audio, push notifications, etc.), we might consider native wrappers. But likely not needed initially. The web stack suffices.

Viable PWA Option: Use the LiveKit web example as a starting point (React + Next.js). This example was specifically built for a similar purpose â€“ a voice AI assistant web frontend[15]. Stripping out LiveKit-specific code and replacing it with direct REST calls might be easier than starting from scratch. Alternatively, use a design kit like Material UI or Tailwind CSS to quickly put together a clean chat interface. The key features to implement: - Chat transcript display (user query and assistant answer, with scrollback). - Mic button to capture audio, stop, and send. - Text input box for alternate input mode. - Play/Pause for the voice response (in case user wants to replay). - Possibly a simple settings menu (e.g. toggle TTS voice on/off, choose persona if multiple, etc.).

Because Rob values structured output, we can incorporate a Markdown renderer that also handles tables and code if needed (since Meneâ€™ might respond with these formats).

Security: Since the PWA will call local resources, we need to handle CORS and authentication. A simple approach: host the PWA and the API on the same machine/URL (e.g. https://assistant.local/), so that CORS isnâ€™t an issue. For access control, if itâ€™s just Rob using it, a basic token or even limiting to LAN access might suffice. If itâ€™s eventually multi-user (Phase 3 Beta in roadmap[16]), weâ€™d add login and user-specific keys.

3.4 Stack Integration Map

Below is a mapping of the key stack components, their current status, and how theyâ€™ll integrate into the PWA solution:

Implement via prompt strategies. e.g., allow user to ask â€œBonnyâ€ or â€œSteveâ€ specifically, or have Meneâ€™ internally consult them (chain-of-thought). This can be done with a single model using role-play prompts, avoiding complex multi-model syncing. In future, could spin off separate model instances if beneficial.

This map shows the consolidation: moving from three UIs to one, from multiple AI pipelines to one orchestrated service, and eliminating external APIs in favor of local modules.

4. Scalable Assistant Interface & PWA Viability

A PWA-based solution is well-suited to scalability and cross-platform use. Rob can access his assistant from any device with a browser, while the heavy computations remain on a server he controls (his PC or VM).

Scalability Considerations:
- Performance: The most computationally heavy parts (LLM, OpenVoice TTS, Whisper STT) will run on dedicated hardware. If usage grows (e.g., more users or more concurrent tasks), the backend should be containerized or orchestrated to scale. Because the plan is to eventually serve free student access via a sustainable model, we should design with scalability in mind. For instance, the LocalAI and memory service could be deployed in a Docker/Kubernetes setup on cloud infrastructure for more users. The PWA would remain the client. - Stateless vs Stateful: The PWA can manage conversation state (displaying past messages), but the backend should also maintain context (via vector memory and perhaps short-term conversation window). Using vector memory means the system is less dependent on long prompt history (old conversations can be recalled as needed). This is a scalable approach to long-term memory â€“ store everything in a database and retrieve relevant bits per query, rather than always sending a huge context to the LLM. - Multi-language / Modality: OpenVoice allows multi-language speech generation; Whisper can transcribe multiple languages. This opens future capabilities (for example, responding in Zulu or Afrikaans if needed for local users). The architecture is flexible to add such features without redesign. - Offline Use: While the full pipeline canâ€™t run on a low-power device yet, the system can degrade gracefully. If the user is on a powerful laptop, they could run a local instance of everything (truly offline). If on mobile, they connect to the server. The PWA can detect if the backend is unreachable and possibly fall back to limited offline mode (perhaps using the browserâ€™s built-in speech recognition or synthesis as a very rough fallback, or simply queue requests). This ensures resilience.

Viability of PWA vs Native: Given modern web capabilities, a PWA is highly viable for this assistant: - Microphone access: supported in browsers. - Audio playback: supported. - Background operation: limited (browser needs to be open for long interactions, but thatâ€™s acceptable for now). - Notifications: we can use Web Push if we want the assistant to send proactive alerts (not in scope yet, but possible if, say, an automation is done). - Installation: as mentioned, ease of installing on all platforms is a big plus. We avoid maintaining separate codebases for Android and desktop.

5. Risk Points and Potential Blockers

Finally, itâ€™s important to identify risks or blockers in implementing and operating this system:

Hardware & Performance: Running OpenVoice (TTS) and a large LLM (like 13B or 70B parameters) simultaneously is resource-intensive. If Robâ€™s local workstation has limited GPU or if itâ€™s also being used for other tasks, performance might suffer. This could lead to slow response times, which hurt the user experience (especially for voice where latency is noticeable). Mitigation: Optimize model sizes (e.g., use 7B LLM for most queries, only use bigger models for complex ones; use quantized models), and consider using the cloud VM for heavy jobs (the hybrid local/cloud approach[17][18] could offload tasks to GCP when needed). Also, implement caching: frequently needed info from memory can be pre-fetched or summarized to reduce repeated heavy searches.

Complexity of Integration: Building a custom pipeline (even with existing libraries) is non-trivial. Debugging issues across STT -> LLM -> RAG -> TTS could be challenging. For example, if answers start getting irrelevant, is it the retrieval failing or the LLM ignoring the context? Also, things like audio encoding issues or CORS problems can crop up. We should allocate time for end-to-end testing and use the eval approach from LiveKit (writing tests for various scenarios) to catch regressions. This addresses the past issue where failures werenâ€™t caught until Rob was frustrated. Regular metrics and logs (as LiveKitâ€™s example did[19]) should be included so we can monitor latency of each step, errors, etc.

Redundant Components Not Fully Retired: One risk is partially transitioning and ending up with a messy stack. For instance, if the PWA is deployed but Rob also continues using OpenWebUI or the old LiveKit pipeline in parallel, improvements in one might not reflect in the other. Itâ€™s best to commit to the unified solution once itâ€™s ready, to focus efforts. We should migrate any remaining unique features from the old tools into the new stack so nothing is lost (for example, if OpenWebUI had a nice way of doing X, replicate it in the PWA).

Data Consistency & Privacy: Ensuring the local memory (ChromaDB) and any cloud usage remain in sync and secure. The Hybrid Local/Cloud RAG document discussed methods like VPN or secure tunnels[20][21]. If the cloud agent (Gemini) needs to access local data, we must implement one of those (SSH tunnel, sync to a cloud store, etc.). This is a project in itself â€“ for now, we might focus on local usage, but if cloud LLMs are used, avoid sending any private data to them except via controlled retrieval (e.g., only send relevant snippets, not entire databases). We should also guard the local API â€“ if this will be accessible over the internet for Robâ€™s use on the go, secure it behind VPN or at least strong auth to prevent unauthorized access to his data or AI agent.

Maintenance & Updates: The AI field moves fast. OpenVoice is quite new (and could have updates), LocalAI is under active development. Updates could introduce changes. For example, new versions of models or new features (like OpenAIâ€™s function calling, etc.) might be desirable to integrate later. We should modularize the system so components can be upgraded without breaking everything. Using Docker for deployment can pin working versions and be updated in a controlled way. Also, documenting the entire setup is crucial so that if something crashes, we know which piece to look at (preventing the scenario of chaotic debugging).

User Experience Tuning: A risk less technical but important: the assistantâ€™s behavior. Robâ€™s â€œTruth Modeâ€ and persona parameters are carefully designed. We must ensure the new system preserves these. When we switch from GPT-4 to a local model, we might lose some of the finesse and knowledge. The local model will need fine system prompts (including all those instructions about honesty, tone, etc. from Meneâ€™s profile). It might even help to fine-tune a local model on some of Rob and Meneâ€™s conversations to imbue it with the style â€“ although thatâ€™s optional. Thereâ€™s a risk that initial outputs disappoint in quality or style, which could erode Robâ€™s trust again. We mitigate this by extensive testing with Robâ€™s typical questions and reviewing the outputs. If needed, we use the cloud model for important contexts (since GPT-4 and future Gemini have higher quality) â€“ basically a â€œquality-of-serviceâ€ consideration.

OpenVoice Model Size: Another practical blocker could be the size of OpenVoice models or need for a lengthy voice sample. If the model requires a long sample or a lot of VRAM, there might be initial hurdles. However, the community examples show itâ€™s feasible to run (many have done so on consumer GPUs following tutorials). Weâ€™ll allocate time to get it running and possibly convert the model to a more efficient format if needed (like ONNX or half-precision) to ensure it can run concurrently with the LLM.

Despite these risks, the plan leverages robust, well-supported open-source components and is in line with modern AI architecture practices. The benefits (persistent memory, no cloud lock-in, personalized voice, unified UI) outweigh the challenges, and each risk has a reasonable mitigation path.
